<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequence of star fruit ( Averrhoa carambola )"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-06-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Oxalidaceae is one of the most important plant families in horticulture, and its key commercially relevant genus, Averrhoa, has diverse growth habits and fruit types. Here, we describe the assembly of a high-quality chromosome-scale genome sequence for Averrhoa carambola (star fruit). Ks distribution analysis showed that A. carambola underwent a whole-genome triplication event, i.e., the gamma event shared by most eudicots. Comparisons between A. carambola and other angiosperms also permitted the generation of Oxalidaceae gene annotations. We identified unique gene families and analyzed gene family expansion and contraction. This analysis revealed significant changes in MADS-box gene family content, which might be related to the cauliflory of A. carambola. In addition, we identified and analyzed a total of 204 nucleotide-binding site, leucine-rich repeat receptor (NLR) genes and 58 WRKY genes in the genome, which may be related to the defense response. Our results provide insights into the origin, evolution and diversification of star fruit."/>

data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Donghui-Peng" data-author-popup="auth-Donghui-Peng">Donghui Peng</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Fujian Agriculture and Forestry University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1760 2876, grid.256111.0, Key Laboratory of National Forestry and Grassland Administration for Orchid Conservation and Utilization at College of Landscape             <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-4580-2114"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-4580-2114</a></span><sup             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Oxalidaceae is one of the most important plant families in horticulture, and its key commercially relevant genus, <i>Averrhoa</i>, has diverse growth habits and fruit types. Here, we describe the assembly of a high-quality chromosome-scale genome sequence for <i>Averrhoa carambola</i> (star fruit). <i>Ks</i> distribution analysis showed that <i>A. carambola</i> underwent a whole-genome triplication event, i.e., the gamma event shared by most eudicots. Comparisons between <i>A. carambola</i> and other angiosperms also permitted the generation of Oxalidaceae gene annotations. We identified unique gene families and analyzed gene family expansion and contraction. This analysis revealed significant changes in MADS-box gene family content, which might be related to the cauliflory of <i>A. carambola</i>. In addition, we identified and analyzed a total of 204 nucleotide-binding site, leucine-rich repeat receptor (NLR) genes and 58 WRKY genes in the genome, which may be related to the defense response. Our results provide insights into the origin, evolution and diversification of star fruit.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Wood sorrel (Oxalidaceae family) includes approximately 780 species and is distributed in both tropical and temperate areas. It contains species with various forms, including herbs, shrubs, and trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Veldkamp, J. F. in Flora Malesiana (ed. van Steenis, C. G. C. J.) 155–178 (Noordhoff, Leyden, 1971)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d580e743">1</a></sup>. Wood sorrels are important economic crops and are utilized for both ornamental decoration and medicinal applications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Khare, C. P. Indian Medicinal Plants 74–75 (Springer, New York, 2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d580e747">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gunawardena, D. C., Jayasinghe, L. &amp; Fujimoto, Y. Phytotoxic constituents of the fruits of Averrhoa carambola. Chem. Nat. Compd. 51, 532–533 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d580e750">3</a></sup>. Based on morphological and molecular data, Oxalidaceae belongs to Oxalidales and is sister to the Connaraceae family. It can be divided into two main subfamilies: Oxalidoideae and Averrhooideae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="The Angiosperm Phylogeny Group. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1–20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d580e754">4</a></sup>. Averrhooideae differs from Oxalidoideae, an herbaceous subfamily, by having woody plants. It has four genera and is classified into two tribes: Biophyteae (<i>Biophytum</i>) and Averrhoeae (<i>Dapania</i>, <i>Averrhoa</i> and <i>Sarcotheca</i>). It is mostly distributed in tropical and subtropical regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Stevens, P. F. Angiosperm Phylogeny Website. Version 14, July 2017 [and more or less continuously updated since] (2001 onwards)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d580e771">5</a></sup>. Although these genera share synapomorphies with other wood sorrels, such as floral morphology, <i>Averrhoa</i> possesses several unique traits, including imparipinnate leaves, an herbaceous to papyraceous form, lateral petiolules that do not leave a stalk on the rachis after dropping, and the presence of 3–7 ovules per locule<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Veldkamp, J. F. in Flora Malesiana (ed. van Steenis, C. G. C. J.) 155–178 (Noordhoff, Leyden, 1971)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d580e778">1</a></sup>. Therefore, <i>Averrhoa</i> is a key taxon in the evolutionary assessment of wood sorrel structure, and analysis of its genome should reveal new insights into the key adaptations that contribute to the diversification within Oxalidaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Heibl, C. &amp; Renner, S. S. Distribution models and a dated phylogeny for Chilean Oxalis species reveal occupation of new habitats by different lineages, not rapid adaptive radiation. Syst. Biol. 61, 823–834 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d580e786">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Sun, M., Naeem, R., Su, J. X., Cao, Z. Y. &amp; Chen, Z. D. Phylogeny of the Rosidae: a dense taxon sampling analysis. J. Syst. Evol. 54, 363–391 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d580e789">7</a></sup>.</p><p><i>Averrhoa carambola</i>, known as star fruit, originated in Asia and has been cultivated in Southeast Asia and Malaysia for many centuries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fieischmann, P., Watanabe, N. &amp; Winterhalter, P. Enzymatic carotenoid cleavage in star fruit (Averrhoa carambola). Phytochemistry 63, 131–137 (2003)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d580e798">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Neto, M. M. et al. Intoxication by star fruit (Averrhoa carambola) in 32 uraemic patients: treatment and outcome. Nephrol. Dial. Transplant. 18, 120–125 (2003)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d580e798_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Carolino, R. O. G. et al. Convulsant activity and neurochemical alterations induced by a fraction obtained from fruit Averrhoa carambola (Oxalidaceae: Geraniales). Neurochem. Int. 46, 523–531 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d580e801">10</a></sup>. Because the flesh is juicy and rich in vitamin C, star fruit is a commonly consumed tropical fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Shui, G. G. &amp; Leong, L. P. Residue from star fruit as valuable source for functional food ingredients and antioxidant nutraceuticals. Food Chem. 97, 277–284 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d580e805">11</a></sup>. In China, the total consumption of star fruit is approximately 2.6 million tons per year, while the annual production of star fruit in China is approximately two million tons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Shui, G. G. &amp; Leong, L. P. Residue from star fruit as valuable source for functional food ingredients and antioxidant nutraceuticals. Food Chem. 97, 277–284 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d580e809">11</a></sup>. In addition, star fruit is widely cultivated as a street tree in southern Chinese cities due to its dense foliage and star-like fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Bin-Ngah, A.W., Ahmad, I. &amp; Hassan, A. Carambola Production, Processing and Marketing in Malaysia (Inter-American Society for Tropical Horticulture (IASTH), 1992)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d580e813">12</a></sup>. Furthermore, the characteristics of bearing flowers and fruits on the main trunk and flowering year-round when temperatures exceed 27 °C in tropical regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Khoo, H. et al. A review on underutilized tropical fruits in Malaysia. Guangxi Agr. Sci. 41, 698–702 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d580e817">13</a></sup> make it an ideal species with which to explore economic and interesting traits (cauliflory, defined as flowering from the lower branch and trunk areas of woody plants, and high yield) at the whole-genome scale.</p><p><i>Cephalotus follicularis</i> is a carnivorous plant native to southwest Australia that belongs to the monospecific family Cephalotaceae and is the only species of the order Oxalidales with a sequenced and annotated genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Fukushima, K. et al. Genome of the pitcher plant Cephalotus reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d580e826">14</a></sup>. The relationship between star fruit and <i>Cephalotus follicularis</i> is not currently known and could be better understood through a comparison of their sequenced genomes.</p><p>Here, we present a complete genome sequence for <i>A. carambola</i>. Comparisons of genomic data with those from other flowering plants provide fundamental insights into the origin, evolution, adaptation, and diversification of star fruit.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and characterization</h3><p><i>A. carambola</i> has a karyotype of 2<i>N</i> = 2X = 22 chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wu, Q. Study on Technology of Breeding polyploid of Averrhoa Carambola by Biotechnique. Master dissertation. Southwest Agricultural University, Chongqing (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d580e856">15</a></sup>. To sequence its genome, we utilized Illumina HiSeq short reads. We obtained a total of 131 Gb of raw reads with short inserts after library construction and sequencing (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">1</a>). The <i>A. carambola</i> genome was estimated to be 357.79 Mb in size with a heterozygosity of 1.15% based on 17-<i>K</i>-<i>mer</i> analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">1</a>). Next, we utilized Oxford Nanopore Technology (ONT) and obtained a total of 52.33 Gb of long reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">1</a>). The ONT reads were corrected and assembled to produce a 335.49 Mb genome with a contig N50 size of 4.22 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">2</a>). Then, the draft assembly was polished using short reads, and BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs, v3.1.0) assessment indicated that the completeness of the genome was 96.30%, suggesting that the <i>A. carambola</i> genome is nearly complete and of high quality (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>We additionally used 42.76 Gb of Hi-C clean data to reconstruct physical maps by reordering and clustering the assembled scaffolds. We anchored 90.88% of the assembly (305.13 Mb) onto 11 pseudochromosomes using a hierarchical clustering strategy (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">4</a>). Chromatin interaction data were used to assess the quality of the Hi-C assembly, which indicated that the assembly was of high quality (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">2</a>). The length of the pseudochromosomes ranged from 17.89 Mb to 33.98 Mb with an N50 value of 31.25 Mb (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>Approximately 61.3% of the <i>A. carambola</i> genome was found to be composed of repetitive elements (transposon elements, TEs) (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">6</a>). We confidently annotated 25,419 protein-coding genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">5</a>), of which 21,316 (83.86%) were supported by transcriptome data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">8</a>). A total of 94.8% of annotated BUSCO gene models were identified, suggesting the near completeness of gene prediction (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">8</a>). In addition, we identified 86 microRNAs, 581 transfer RNAs, 71 ribosomal RNAs and 212 small nuclear RNAs in the <i>A. carambola</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Evolution of gene families</h3><p>We then constructed a high-confidence phylogenetic tree and estimated the divergence times of 24 different plant species based on genes extracted from a total of 93 single-copy families (see Methods and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">10</a>). The estimated divergence time of this set of Oxalidaceae species was 102 Mya (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">6</a>). Next, we determined the expansion and contraction of orthologous gene families using CAFÉ 4.2 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">7</a>). Thirty-three gene families were expanded in the lineage leading to Oxalidales, whereas 904 families were contracted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig1">1</a>). Four hundred ninety gene families were expanded in <i>A. carambola</i>, while 1021 gene families were contracted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The expansion and contraction of gene families."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: The expansion and contraction of gene families.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="870" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The green number indicates the number of expanded gene families, while the red number indicates the number of contracted gene families.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>By comparing 24 different plant species, 504 gene families, including 8153 <i>A. carambola</i> genes, appeared to be unique to carambola (see Methods, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">10</a>). We performed GO and KEGG enrichment analysis (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">11</a>) and found that these gene families were enriched in several categories (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">12</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">17</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Collinearity analysis</h3><p>Genes are typically conserved both in function and order inside collinear fragments among closely related species. We utilized MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wang, C. et al. Genome-wide analysis of local chromatin packing in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Res. 25, 246 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d580e1020">16</a></sup> to assess the collinearity among species related to <i>Averrhoa carambola</i> and found that all the predicted genes except TE-related genes were highly conserved in both function and order (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">9</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Collinear analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Collinear analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1383" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Circos collinearity plot of <i>A. carambola</i>. (<b>b</b>) Circos collinearity plot of <i>A. carambola</i> with <i>Vitis vinifera.</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Whole-genome duplication</h3><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) is a process of genome doubling that dramatically increases genome complexity. One of the striking features of plant genomes is that WGD has occurred many times<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738–749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d580e1079">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Van de Peer, Y., Maere, S. &amp; Meyer, A. The evolutionary significance of ancient genome duplications. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 725–732 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d580e1082">18</a></sup>. WGD is a particularly important feature of angiosperm genomes. To determine whether the <i>Averrhoa</i> genome had undergone WGD during evolution, we used <i>Ks</i> (synonymous substitutions per site) distribution analysis. The paralogous gene pairs were extracted from the OrthoMCL results, and the <i>Ks</i> values were calculated using CodeML in the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wang, C. et al. Genome-wide analysis of local chromatin packing in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Res. 25, 246 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d580e1095">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K. H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667–1678 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d580e1098">19</a></sup>.</p><p>There was a peak between <i>Ks</i> values of 1.6–1.8, indicating that the <i>A. carambola</i> genome had undergone a WGD event. Further analysis of <i>A. carambola</i> and <i>C. follicularis</i> revealed that the common ancestor prior to the differentiation of <i>Averrhoa</i> and <i>C. follicularis</i> experienced a WGD event. This event was likely the γ event shared by most core eudicots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig3">3</a>). Comparative genomic analysis between the <i>A. carambola</i> and <i>C. follicularis</i> genomes revealed a one-to-one syntenic relationship, suggesting that no WGD events occurred after speciation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Ks distribution of Averrhoa carambola."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: <i>Ks</i> distribution of <i>Averrhoa carambola</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1168" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) <i>Ks</i> distribution of <i>A. carambola</i> paralogous genes. (<b>b</b>) <i>Ks</i> distribution of <i>A. carambola</i> and <i>Cephalotus follicularis.</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">MADS-box genes of star fruit</h3><p>MADS-box genes are known to be involved in many important processes during plant development and are especially known for their roles in flowering and flower development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orcid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65–72 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d580e1192">20</a></sup>. Because <i>Averrhoa</i> is well known for its cauliflory, defined as flowering from the lower branch and trunk areas of woody plants, we focused on identifying and characterizing the MADS-box genes in the <i>Averrhoa</i> genome in more detail.</p><p>In total, 74 putative functional MADS-box genes and two pseudogenes were identified in <i>A. carambola</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab1">1</a>). This number is less than the number of MADS-box genes found in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Parenicova, L. et al. Molecular and phylogenetic analyses of the complete MADS-box transcription factor family in Arabidopsis: new openings to the MADS world. Plant Cell 15, 1538–1551 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d580e1213">21</a></sup> and <i>Theobroma cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Argout, X. et al. The cacao Criollo genome v2.0: an improved version of the genome for genetic and functional genomic studies. BMC Genomics 18, 730 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d580e1219">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d580e1222">23</a></sup> but greater than the number found in <i>C. follicularis</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab1">1</a>). <i>A. carambola</i> has 48 type-II MADS-box genes, which is comparable to the number in <i>A. thaliana</i> (45) but less than the number found in <i>T. cacao</i> (67) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab1">1</a>). Phylogenetic analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">12</a>) showed that many of the type-II MADS-box genes were duplicated, except those in the B-class, PI, FLC, AGL12, and Bs clades. The duplicated type-II clades included A-class (three members), B-class AP3 (two members), C/D-class (three members), E-class (four members), AGL6 (two members), SOC1 (three members), AGL15 (two members), ANR1 (five members), SVP (15 members), and MICK* (five members) clades (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab1">1</a>). Notably, we found that the SVP (15 members) clade in <i>A. carambola</i> contained more genes than that in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. follicularis</i> and cocoa tree (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab1">1</a>). The large number of SVP clade members might be due to tandem duplication. In <i>Arabidopsis</i>, the two <i>SVP</i> paralogs <i>SVP</i> and <i>AGL24</i> are involved in floral transition and development. <i>SVP</i> suppresses flowering by acting with <i>FLC</i> to negatively regulate <i>SOC1</i> and <i>FT</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Willmann, M. R. &amp; Poethig, R. S. The effect of the floral repressor FLC on the timing and progression of vegetative phase change in Arabidopsis. Development 138, 677–685 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d580e1289">24</a></sup>. In contrast, <i>AGL24</i> acts as a flowering activator to activate <i>SOC1</i> expression during inflorescence development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Gregis, V., Sessa, A., Dorca-Fornell, C. &amp; Kater, M. M. The Arabidopsis floral meristem identity genes AP1, AGL24 and SVP directly repress class B and C floral homeotic genes. Plant J. 60, 626–637 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d580e1299">25</a></sup>. We detected transcripts of all <i>SVP</i>-like genes except <i>Yangtao2024516</i> in vegetative leaves and shoots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig4">4</a>). Interestingly, expression of an <i>SVP</i>-like gene (<i>Yangtao2024516</i>) was detected in the inflorescence and flower buds (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig4">4</a>). These results suggested that <i>Yangtao2024516</i> functions in flowering activation, and the other 14 <i>SVP</i>-like genes might also be related to flowering suppression. The expanded <i>SVP</i> clade, including members with differential expression patterns in <i>A. carambola</i>, might contribute to flowering regulatory networks and relate to <i>A. carambola</i> cauliflory.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 MADS-box genes in <i>Averrhoa carambola, Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Cephalotus follicularis</i> and <i>Theobroma cacao.</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Expression patterns of genes involved in the flowering."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Expression patterns of genes involved in the flowering.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1577" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Pathway of <i>Averrhoa carambola.</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Cauliflorous flowering occurs due to the presence of adventitious buds that remain dormant and take years to develop after their formation, when the trunk develops and thickens due to secondary growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Oliveira, G. P. et al. Origin and development of reproductive buds in jabuticaba cv. Sabará (Plinia jaboticaba Vell). Sci. Horticulturae 249, 432–438 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d580e2330">26</a></sup>. It has been reported that <i>SVP2</i> functions in the suppression of meristem activity to prevent precocious budbreak in the perennial species kiwifruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wu, R. Kiwifruit SVP2, a dormancy regulator. Eur. J. Hort. Sci. 83, 231–235 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d580e2337">27</a></sup>. The flowering repressor <i>SVP</i> is also controlled by the autonomous pathway and directly represses <i>SOC1</i> transcription in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li, D. et al. A repressor complex governs the integration of flowering signals in Arabidopsis. Dev. Cell. 15, 110–120 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d580e2350">28</a></sup>. Interestingly, we found that genes involved in the autonomous flowering time pathway might repress the expression of <i>SVP</i>-like genes in reproductive tissues to promote flowering through activation of the floral integrator genes <i>FLOWERING LOCUS T</i> and <i>SOC1</i> (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig4">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Fig5">5</a>). Further study on the <i>A. carambola</i> flowering time pathways will improve our knowledge about causal genes, with applications in commercial variety selection.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Putative autonomous flowering."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Putative autonomous flowering.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0307-3/MediaObjects/41438_2020_307_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="548" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Time pathway in <i>Averrhoa carambola.</i></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Compared with type-II MADS-box genes, only 26 putative functional type-I genes and two pseudogenes were characterized (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab1">1</a>), which may have been due to the low duplication rate and high loss rate of type-I MADS-box genes. Tandem duplications seem to have contributed to more type-I genes, especially those in the Mα group. This suggests that type-I MADS-box genes have mainly been duplicated during smaller-scale and more-recent duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Parenicova, L. et al. Molecular and phylogenetic analyses of the complete MADS-box transcription factor family in Arabidopsis: new openings to the MADS world. Plant Cell 15, 1538–1551 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d580e2401">21</a></sup>. Functional studies of type-I MADS-box genes in <i>A. carambola</i> will advance our understanding of their role in Oxalidaceae.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Analysis of NLR genes and the WRKY gene family</h3><p>Resistance (R) genes produce R proteins to provide plant disease resistance against pathogens, most of which are nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat (NLR) genes. NLRs contain three classes based on their N-terminal domain structures, namely, the toll/interleukin-1 receptor (TIR) NLR (TNL), coiled-coil (CC) NLR (CNL), and resistance to powdery mildew8 (RPW8) NLR (RLN)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Shao, Z. et al. Large-scale analyses of angiosperm nucleotide-binding site-leucine-rich repeat genes reveal three anciently diverged classes with distinct evolutionary patterns. Plant Physiol. 170, 2095–2109 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d580e2416">29</a></sup>. Here, a genome-wide identification of NLR genes in star fruit was carried out, resulting in the identification of 204 NLR genes, including homologues of known resistance genes: RNL (with 13 members), TNL (with 41 members) and CNL (with 150 members) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab2">2</a>). There seem to be more NLR genes in <i>A. carambola</i> than in <i>A. thaliana</i> (174 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Maleck, K. et al. The transcriptome of Arabidopsis thaliana during systemic acquired resistance. Nat. Genet. 26, 403–410 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d580e2429">30</a></sup> but fewer than in the basal angiosperm <i>Nymphaea colorata</i> (342 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Zhang, L. S. et al. The water lily genome and the early evolution of flowering plants. Nature 577, 79–84 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d580e2437">31</a></sup>. Phylogenetic analysis of the NLR genes indicated that tandem duplication contributed to the increasing number of CNL genes. Remarkably, the NLR genes in the <i>C. follicularis</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Fukushima, K. et al. Genome of the pitcher plant Cephalotus reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d580e2444">14</a></sup> showed sharp contraction after the speciation of <i>A. carambola</i> and <i>C. follicularis</i>, with only one RNL and one TNL identified (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 NLR genes in <i>Averrhoa carambola, Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Cephalotus follicularis</i> and <i>Nymphaea colorata.</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Plant WRKY proteins are transcription factors (TFs) involved in regulating plant growth and development, as well as responses to many biotic and abiotic stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Phukan, U. et al. WRKY transcription factors: molecular regulation and stress responses in plants. Front Plant Sci. 7, 760 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d580e2683">32</a></sup>. An investigation of the star fruit genome revealed 58 WRKY genes. There appear to be fewer WRKY genes in star fruit than in the model plant <i>A. thaliana</i> (72 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang, Q., Wang, M., Zhang, X., Hao, B., Kaushik, S. K. &amp; Pan, Y. WRKY gene family evolution in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetica 139, 973 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d580e2690">33</a></sup> and the basal angiosperm <i>N. colorata</i> (69 genes)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Zhang, L. S. et al. The water lily genome and the early evolution of flowering plants. Nature 577, 79–84 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d580e2697">31</a></sup>. The closely related species <i>C. follicularis</i> contains 42 WRKY genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Fukushima, K. et al. Genome of the pitcher plant Cephalotus reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d580e2705">14</a></sup> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#Tab3">3</a>). Phylogenetic analysis showed that WRKY genes, especially type-IIc and type-I genes, underwent expansion in <i>A. carambola</i>, with the exception of those in the type-IIa clade. Among the 58 WRKY genes, type I (with 10 members), type II (33 members) and type III (eight members) contained more members than in <i>C. follicularis</i> (six members in type I, 29 members in type II and seven members in type III) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">14</a>). Interestingly, there was an unknown clade present in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. follicularis</i>, which contained three members in the star fruit genome and two members in the <i>N. colorata</i> genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 WRKY genes in <i>Averrhoa carambola, Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Cephalotus follicularis</i> and <i>Nymphaea colorata.</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Although star fruit is well known as a delicacy in the tropics, research on it has been hampered by the absence of genetic resources. We identified new gene families and analyzed gene family expansion and contraction by comparisons with related species. Significant changes were found in the MADS-box gene family, which might be related to the cauliflory of <i>A. carambola</i>. Evolutionary analysis revealed one WGD event, which was the γ event experienced by most eudicots. The <i>A. carambola</i> assembly represents the first from the wood sorrel family and thus provides a valuable resource for evolutionary phylogenomic studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Material and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Material and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant sample preparation and sequencing</h3><p>Diploid <i>A. carambola</i> was cultivated at the South China Botanical Garden, Guangzhou, Guangdong Province, China. Fresh young leaves were collected to extract genomic DNA for 400 bp paired-end library construction and Illumina sequencing. Genome size and heterozygosity were calculated using KmerFreq and GCE based on a 17-<i>K-mer</i> distribution. The high-molecular-weight DNA of fresh young leaves was extracted and randomly fragmented to construct a 20 kb library for Oxford Nanopore sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genome assembly and chromosome anchoring</h3><p>The raw fastq data from ONT sequencing were transformed and filtered using MinKNOW software. Canu (v1.7) was used to correct and trim the raw ONT reads with the default parameters. The corrected and trimmed ONT reads were assembled by SMARTdenovo using 21-mers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Schmidt, M. H. W. et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell 29, 2336–2348 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d580e3136">34</a></sup>. The assembled contigs were then polished three times with Pilon (v1.22) using Illumina short reads. The quality of the genome assembly was estimated by searching for BUSCO v3.1.0 using the Embryophyta ODB 10 database.</p><p>Fresh leaves of <i>A. carambola</i> were used to construct a Hi-C sequencing library, including chromatin crosslinking, chromatin digestion with HindIII, biotin labeling and end repair, DNA purification and streptavidin pull-down of labeled Hi-C ligation products. The library was then sequenced using the Illumina platform, and the clean sequences were mapped to the draft genome, with valid Hi-C reads employed to correct the draft assembly. Finally, the draft genome of <i>A. carambola</i> was assembled into chromosomes using Lachesis (9).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Identification of repetitive sequences</h3><p>Tandem repeats across the genome were predicted using Tandem Repeats Finder (v4.07b, <a href="http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html">http://tandem.bu.edu/trf/trf.html</a>). Transposable elements (TEs) were first identified using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>, v3.3.0) and RepeatProteinMask based on the Repbase TE library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d580e3171">35</a></sup>. Next, two de novo predication software programs, RepeatModeler (<a href="http://repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html">http://repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler.html</a>) and LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d580e3182">36</a></sup>, were used to identify TEs in the star fruit genome. Finally, repeat sequences with identities ≥ 50% were grouped into the same class.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Three independent methods were used for gene prediction. Homologous sequence searching was performed by comparing protein sequences of five sequenced species against the star fruit genome using the TBLASTN algorithm with a cut-off E-value of ≤ 1e<sup>−5</sup>. Then, the corresponding homologous genome sequences were aligned against BLAST hits using GeneWise v2.4.1 to extract accurate exon–intron information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d580e3196">37</a></sup>. Three <i>ab initio</i> prediction software programs, Augustus v3.0.2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Stanke, M., Schoffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinform. 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d580e3203">38</a></sup>, FGENESH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Salamov, A. A. &amp; Solovyev, V. V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516–522 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d580e3207">39</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d580e3212">40</a></sup>, were employed for de novo gene prediction. Then, the homology-based and <i>ab initio</i> gene structures were merged into a nonredundant gene model using GLEAN. The completeness of gene prediction was evaluated using BUSCO v3.1.0. Finally, the RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq) reads were mapped to the assembly using TopHat v2.0.11<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Trapnell, C., Pachter, L. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TopHat: discovering splice junctions with RNA-Seq. Bioinformatics 25, 1105–1111 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d580e3219">41</a></sup>, and Cufflinks v2.2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-Seq. experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d580e3223">42</a></sup> was applied to combine mapping results for transcript structural predictions.</p><p>The protein sequences of the consensus gene set were aligned to various protein databases, including GO (The Gene Ontology Consortium), KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ogata, H. et al. KEGG: Kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 29–34 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d580e3230">43</a></sup>, InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Hunter, S. et al. InterPro: the integrative protein signature database. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, D211–215 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d580e3234">44</a></sup>, Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL, for the annotation of predicted genes. The rRNAs were identified by aligning the rRNA template sequences from the Rfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Griffiths, J. S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, D121–124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d580e3238">45</a></sup> database against the genome using the BLASTN algorithm at an E-value cut-off of 1e<sup>−5</sup>. The tRNAs were predicted using tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d580e3244">46</a></sup>, and other ncRNAs were predicted by running Infernal 0.81 software against the Rfam database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Genome evolution analysis</h3><p>Gene families present in the 24 genomes were identified using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d580e3257">47</a></sup>. Peptide sequences from 93 single-copy gene families were used to construct phylogenetic relationships and estimate divergence times. Alignments from MUSCLE were then converted to coding sequences. Fourfold degenerate sites were concatenated and used to estimate the neutral substitution rate per year and the divergence time. PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d580e3261">48</a></sup> was used to construct a phylogenetic tree. The Bayesian relaxed molecular clock approach was used to estimate species divergence times using the program MCMCTREE v4.0, which is part of the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d580e3265">49</a></sup>. The ‘correlated molecular clock’ and ‘JC69’ models were used. Published <i>Arabidopsis</i>–<i>Papaya</i> (54–90 Mya), <i>P. trichocarpa</i>–<i>Arabidopsis</i> (100–129 Mya), monocot–dicot (140 Mya) and angiosperm (&lt;200 Mya) divergence times were used for calibration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Zhang, G. Q. et al. The Apostasia genome and the evolution of orchid. Nature 549, 379–383 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d580e3282">50</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Transcriptome sequencing and expression analysis</h3><p>The inflorescence, floral bud, leaf and shoot were collected independently from <i>A. carambola</i>. Total RNA was extracted according to the manufacturer’s protocol. Illumina RNA-Seq libraries were prepared and sequenced on a HiSeq 2500 system following the manufacturer’s instructions (Illumina, USA). Two biological replicates were analyzed for each sample. To estimate gene expression levels, clean reads of each sample were mapped onto the assembled genome to obtain read counts for each gene using HTSeq-count and normalized to FPKM counts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-Seq. experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d580e3297">42</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Phylogenetic reconstruction of the MADS-box gene family</h3><p>For the identification of MADS-box family members in <i>A. carambola</i>, BLASTp was applied using known plant MADS-box proteins as reference sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Parenicova, L. et al. Molecular and phylogenetic analyses of the complete MADS-box transcription factor family in Arabidopsis: new openings to the MADS world. Plant Cell 15, 1538–1551 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d580e3312">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan-an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d580e3315">51</a></sup>, with an E-value cut-off of ≤ 1e−5. For phylogenetic analyses, a total of 333 protein sequences, including 76 <i>A. carambola</i> AcMADS-box, 106 <i>A. thaliana</i> AtMADS-box<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Parenicova, L. et al. Molecular and phylogenetic analyses of the complete MADS-box transcription factor family in Arabidopsis: new openings to the MADS world. Plant Cell 15, 1538–1551 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d580e3325">21</a></sup>, 53 <i>C. follicularis</i> CfMADS-box<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Fukushima, K. et al. Genome of the pitcher plant Cephalotus reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d580e3333">14</a></sup> and 98 <i>T. cacao</i> TcMADS-box<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Argout, X. et al. The cacao Criollo genome v2.0: an improved version of the genome for genetic and functional genomic studies. BMC Genomics 18, 730 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d580e3340">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d580e3343">23</a></sup>, were aligned using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Letunic, L., Doerks, T. &amp; Bork, P. SMART: recent updates, new develpoments and status in 2015. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D257–D260 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d580e3347">52</a></sup> (v3.8.31; <a href="http://www.drive5.com/muscle">http://www.drive5.com/muscle</a>) with default parameters. A phylogenetic tree was drawn through RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d580e3359">53</a></sup> with the GTRGAMMA substitution model and 1000 bootstraps on the CIPRES website (<a href="https://www.phylo.org/portal2/home.action">https://www.phylo.org/portal2/home.action</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Miller, M. A., Pfeiffer, W. &amp; Schwartz, T. Creating the CIPRES Science Gateway for inference of large phylogenetic trees. 2010 Gateway Computing Environments Workshop (GCE), (ed. Institute of Electrical and Electronic Engineers) 1–8 (New Orleans, LA, 2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d580e3370">54</a></sup>. The phylogenetic tree was visualized using FigTree software (<a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree">http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree</a>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">NLR genes and WRKY TF identification</h3><p>All the annotated protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>C. follicularis</i>, and <i>N. colorata</i> were downloaded. The NLR gene (PF00931) and WRKY TF (PF03106) models were obtained from the Pfam database (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/">http://pfam.xfam.org/</a>). The NLR genes and WRKY TFs were then identified using HMMER v3.2.1. The identified sequences were confirmed and filtered using the UniProt database (<a href="https://www.uniprot.org/">https://www.uniprot.org/</a>) and the NCBI database (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/</a>). Then, MAFFT v7.407 was used to align multiple sequences of candidate proteins with default parameters. A maximum likelihood (ML) phylogenetic tree was constructed for the protein sequences of NLR genes and WRKY TFs from <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Maleck, K. et al. The transcriptome of Arabidopsis thaliana during systemic acquired resistance. Nat. Genet. 26, 403–410 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d580e3425">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang, Q., Wang, M., Zhang, X., Hao, B., Kaushik, S. K. &amp; Pan, Y. WRKY gene family evolution in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetica 139, 973 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d580e3428">33</a></sup>, <i>C. follicularis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Fukushima, K. et al. Genome of the pitcher plant Cephalotus reveals genetic changes associated with carnivory. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 0059 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d580e3434">14</a></sup>, <i>N. colorata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Zhang, L. S. et al. The water lily genome and the early evolution of flowering plants. Nature 577, 79–84 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0307-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d580e3440">31</a></sup> and star fruit by FastTree with default parameters.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The whole-genome sequence data reported in this paper have been deposited in the Genome Warehouse of the National Genomics Data Center, Beijing Institute of Genomics (BIG), Chinese Academy of Sciences, under accession number GWHABKE00000000 and are publicly accessible at <a href="https://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh">https://bigd.big.ac.cn/gwh</a>.</p>
